srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Hans_Holbein_the_Younger_-_Portrait_of_a_Lady%2C_perhaps_Katherine_Howard_%28Royal_Collection%29.JPG/300px-Hans_Holbein_the_Younger_-_Portrait_of_a_Lady%2C_perhaps_Katherine_Howard_%28Royal_Collection%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Hans_Holbein_the_Younger_-_Portrait_of_a_Lady%2C_perhaps_Katherine_Howard_%28Royal_Collection%29.JPG/400px-Hans_Holbein_the_Younger_-_Portrait_of_a_Lady%2C_perhaps_Katherine_Howard_%28Royal_Collection%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1426" data-file-height="1416" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:160px;max-width:160px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Catherine_Parr_from_NPG.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Catherine_Parr_from_NPG.jpg/158px-Catherine_Parr_from_NPG.jpg" decoding="async" width="158" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Catherine_Parr_from_NPG.jpg/237px-Catherine_Parr_from_NPG.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Catherine_Parr_from_NPG.jpg/316px-Catherine_Parr_from_NPG.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3042" /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><i>Miniature Portrait of <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Howard" title="Catherine Howard">Catherine Howard</a></i>, Henry's fifth wife, by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Holbein_the_Younger" title="Hans Holbein the Younger">Hans Holbein the Younger</a>, 1540 (left) and <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Parr" title="Catherine Parr">Catherine Parr</a>, Henry's sixth and last wife (right)</div></div></div></div>
<p>On 28 July 1540 (the same day Cromwell was executed), Henry married the young <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Howard" title="Catherine Howard">Catherine Howard</a>, a first cousin and lady-in-waiting of Anne Boleyn.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> He was absolutely delighted with his new queen, and awarded her the lands of Cromwell and a vast array of jewellery.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Soon after the marriage, however, Queen Catherine had an affair with the courtier <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Culpeper" title="Thomas Culpeper">Thomas Culpeper</a>. She also employed <a href="/wiki/Francis_Dereham" title="Francis Dereham">Francis Dereham</a>, who had previously been informally engaged to her and had an affair with her prior to her marriage, as her secretary. The court was informed of her affair with Dereham whilst Henry was away; they dispatched Thomas Cranmer to investigate, who brought evidence of Queen Catherine's previous affair with Dereham to the king's notice.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Though Henry originally refused to believe the allegations, Dereham confessed. It took another meeting of the council, however, before Henry believed the accusations against Dereham and went into a rage, blaming the council before consoling himself in hunting.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>  When questioned, the queen could have admitted a prior contract to marry Dereham, which would have made her subsequent marriage to Henry invalid, but she instead claimed that Dereham had forced her to enter into an adulterous relationship. Dereham, meanwhile, exposed Queen Catherine's relationship with Culpeper. Culpeper and Dereham were both executed, and Catherine too was beheaded on 13 February 1542.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Shrines_destroyed_and_monasteries_dissolved">Shrines destroyed and monasteries dissolved</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Monasteries" title="Dissolution of the Monasteries">Dissolution of the Monasteries</a></div>
<p>In 1538, the chief minister Thomas Cromwell pursued an extensive campaign against what was termed "idolatry" by the followers of the old religion, culminating in September with the dismantling of the shrine of St. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Becket" title="Thomas Becket">Thomas Becket</a> at <a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Cathedral" title="Canterbury Cathedral">Canterbury</a>. As a consequence, the king was excommunicated by Pope Paul III on 17 December of the same year.<sup id="cite_ref-Scarisbrick361_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scarisbrick361-86">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> In 1540, Henry sanctioned the complete destruction of shrines to saints. In 1542, England's remaining monasteries were all dissolved, and their property transferred to the Crown. <a href="/wiki/Abbot" title="Abbot">Abbots</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prior" title="Prior">priors</a> lost their seats in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>; only archbishops and bishops remained. Consequently, the <a href="/wiki/Lords_Spiritual" title="Lords Spiritual">Lords Spiritual</a>—as members of the clergy with seats in the House of Lords were known—were for the first time outnumbered by the <a href="/wiki/Lords_Temporal" title="Lords Temporal">Lords Temporal</a>.
</p>
<h2><span id="Second_invasion_of_France_and_the_.22Rough_Wooing.22_of_Scotland"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_invasion_of_France_and_the_&quot;Rough_Wooing&quot;_of_Scotland">Second invasion of France and the "Rough Wooing" of Scotland</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rough_Wooing" title="Rough Wooing">Rough Wooing</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Enrique_VIII_de_Inglaterra,_por_Hans_Holbein_el_Joven.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Enrique_VIII_de_Inglaterra%2C_por_Hans_Holbein_el_Joven.jpg/170px-Enrique_VIII_de_Inglaterra%2C_por_Hans_Holbein_el_Joven.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Enrique_VIII_de_Inglaterra%2C_por_Hans_Holbein_el_Joven.jpg/255px-Enrique_VIII_de_Inglaterra%2C_por_Hans_Holbein_el_Joven.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Enrique_VIII_de_Inglaterra%2C_por_Hans_Holbein_el_Joven.jpg/340px-Enrique_VIII_de_Inglaterra%2C_por_Hans_Holbein_el_Joven.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1841" data-file-height="2169" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Enrique_VIII_de_Inglaterra,_por_Hans_Holbein_el_Joven.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Henry in 1540, by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Holbein_the_Younger" title="Hans Holbein the Younger">Hans Holbein the Younger</a></div></div></div>
<p>The 1539 alliance between Francis and Charles had soured, eventually degenerating into renewed war. With Catherine of Aragon and Anne Boleyn dead, relations between Charles and Henry improved considerably, and Henry concluded a secret alliance with the Emperor and decided to enter the <a href="/wiki/Italian_War_of_1542%E2%80%931546" title="Italian War of 1542–1546">Italian War</a> in favour of his new ally. An invasion of France was planned for 1543.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> In preparation for it, Henry moved to eliminate the potential threat of Scotland under the youthful <a href="/wiki/James_V_of_Scotland" title="James V of Scotland">James V</a>. The Scots were defeated at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Solway_Moss" title="Battle of Solway Moss">Battle of Solway Moss</a> on 24 November 1542,<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> and James died on 15 December. Henry now hoped to unite the crowns of England and Scotland by marrying his son Edward to James' successor, <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary</a>. The Scottish Regent <a href="/wiki/James_Hamilton,_Duke_of_Ch%C3%A2tellerault" title="James Hamilton, Duke of Châtellerault">Lord Arran</a> agreed to the marriage in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Greenwich" title="Treaty of Greenwich">Treaty of Greenwich</a> on 1 July 1543, but it was rejected by the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Scotland" title="Parliament of Scotland">Parliament of Scotland</a> on 11 December. The result was eight years of war between England and Scotland, a campaign later dubbed "the <a href="/wiki/Rough_Wooing" title="Rough Wooing">Rough Wooing</a>". Despite several peace treaties, unrest continued in Scotland until Henry's death.<sup id="cite_ref-Elton1977b_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elton1977b-133">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Loades79_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Loades79-134">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite the early success with Scotland, Henry hesitated to invade France, annoying Charles. Henry finally went to France in June 1544 with a two-pronged attack. One force under Norfolk ineffectively besieged <a href="/wiki/Montreuil,_Pas-de-Calais" title="Montreuil, Pas-de-Calais">Montreuil</a>. The other, under Suffolk, <a href="/wiki/Sieges_of_Boulogne_(1544%E2%80%931546)#First_siege" class="mw-redirect" title="Sieges of Boulogne (1544–1546)">laid siege to Boulogne</a>. Henry later took personal command, and Boulogne fell on 18 September 1544.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Elton1977b_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elton1977b-133">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> However, Henry had refused Charles' request to march against Paris. Charles' own campaign fizzled, and he made peace with France that same day.<sup id="cite_ref-Loades79_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Loades79-134">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Henry was left alone against France, unable to make peace. Francis attempted to invade England in the summer of 1545, but reached only the Isle of Wight before being repulsed in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Solent" title="Battle of the Solent">Battle of the Solent</a>. Out of money, France and England signed the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Ardres" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Ardres">Treaty of Camp</a> on 7 June 1546. Henry secured Boulogne for eight years. The city was then to be returned to France for 2 million crowns (£750,000). Henry needed the money; the 1544 campaign had cost £650,000, and England was once again bankrupt.<sup id="cite_ref-Loades79_134-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Loades79-134">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_to_Catherine_Parr">Marriage to Catherine Parr</span></h2>
<p>Henry married his last wife, the wealthy widow <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Parr" title="Catherine Parr">Catherine Parr</a>, in July 1543.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> A reformer at heart, she argued with Henry over religion. Ultimately, Henry remained committed to an idiosyncratic mixture of Catholicism and Protestantism; the reactionary mood which had gained ground following the fall of Cromwell had neither eliminated his Protestant streak nor been overcome by it.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Parr helped reconcile Henry with his daughters, Mary and Elizabeth.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> In 1543, an Act of Parliament put them back in the line of succession after Edward. The same act allowed Henry to determine further succession to the throne in his will.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_decline_and_death">Physical decline and death</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CoffinHenryVIIIStGeorgesChapelWindsor.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/CoffinHenryVIIIStGeorgesChapelWindsor.png/220px-CoffinHenryVIIIStGeorgesChapelWindsor.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/CoffinHenryVIIIStGeorgesChapelWindsor.png/330px-CoffinHenryVIIIStGeorgesChapelWindsor.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5b/CoffinHenryVIIIStGeorgesChapelWindsor.png 2x" data-file-width="393" data-file-height="263" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CoffinHenryVIIIStGeorgesChapelWindsor.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coffins of King Henry VIII (centre, damaged), <a href="/wiki/Jane_Seymour" title="Jane Seymour">Queen Jane</a> (right), <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">King Charles I</a> with a child of <a href="/wiki/Anne,_Queen_of_Great_Britain" title="Anne, Queen of Great Britain">Queen Anne</a> (left), vault under the choir, <a href="/wiki/St_George%27s_Chapel,_Windsor_Castle" title="St George&#39;s Chapel, Windsor Castle">St George's Chapel, Windsor Castle</a>, marked by a stone slab in the floor. 1888 sketch by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Young_Nutt" title="Alfred Young Nutt">Alfred Young Nutt</a>, Surveyor to the Dean and Canons</div></div></div>
<p>Late in life, Henry became <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obese</a>, with a waist measurement of 54 inches (140&#160;cm), and had to be moved about with the help of mechanical inventions. He was covered with painful, <a href="/wiki/Pus" title="Pus">pus</a>-filled <a href="/wiki/Boils" class="mw-redirect" title="Boils">boils</a> and possibly suffered from <a href="/wiki/Gout" title="Gout">gout</a>. His obesity and other medical problems can be traced to the jousting accident in 1536 in which he suffered a leg wound. The accident re-opened and aggravated a previous injury he had sustained years earlier, to the extent that his doctors found it difficult to treat. The <a href="/wiki/Chronic_wound" title="Chronic wound">chronic wound</a> festered for the remainder of his life and became <a href="/wiki/Ulcer_(dermatology)" title="Ulcer (dermatology)">ulcerated</a>, thus preventing him from maintaining the level of physical activity he had previously enjoyed. The jousting accident is also believed to have caused Henry's <a href="/wiki/Mood_swing" title="Mood swing">mood swings</a>, which may have had a dramatic effect on his personality and temperament.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-discovery_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-discovery-142">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The theory that Henry suffered from <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a> has been dismissed by most historians.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Historian Susan Maclean Kybett ascribes his demise to <a href="/wiki/Scurvy" title="Scurvy">scurvy</a>, which is caused by a lack of fresh fruits and vegetables.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Alternatively, his wives' pattern of pregnancies and his mental deterioration have led some to suggest that the king may have been <a href="/wiki/Kell_antigen_system" title="Kell antigen system">Kell positive</a> and suffered from <a href="/wiki/Swyer%E2%80%93James_syndrome" title="Swyer–James syndrome">McLeod syndrome</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-discovery_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-discovery-142">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> According to another study, Henry VIII's history and body morphology may have been the result of <a href="/wiki/Traumatic_brain_injury" title="Traumatic brain injury">traumatic brain injury</a> after his 1536 jousting accident, which in turn led to a neuroendocrine cause of his obesity. This analysis identifies <a href="/wiki/Growth_hormone_deficiency" title="Growth hormone deficiency">growth hormone deficiency</a> (GHD) as the source for his increased adiposity but also significant behavioural changes noted in his later years, including his multiple marriages.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Henry's obesity hastened his death at the age of 55, which occurred on 28 January 1547 in the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Whitehall" title="Palace of Whitehall">Palace of Whitehall</a>, on what would have been his father's 90th birthday. The tomb he had planned (with components taken from the tomb intended for <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wolsey" title="Thomas Wolsey">Cardinal Wolsey</a>) was only partly constructed and would never be completed. (The sarcophagus and its base were later removed and used for <a href="/wiki/Horatio_Nelson,_1st_Viscount_Nelson" title="Horatio Nelson, 1st Viscount Nelson">Lord Nelson</a>'s  tomb in the crypt of <a href="/wiki/St._Paul%27s_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Paul&#39;s Cathedral">St. Paul's Cathedral</a>.)<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Henry was interred in a vault at <a href="/wiki/St_George%27s_Chapel,_Windsor_Castle" title="St George&#39;s Chapel, Windsor Castle">St George's Chapel, Windsor Castle</a>, next to Jane Seymour.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Over a hundred years later, <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">King Charles I</a> (1625–1649) was buried in the same vault.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Succession">Succession</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Third_Succession_Act" title="Third Succession Act">Third Succession Act</a></div>
<p>Upon Henry's death, he was succeeded by his son <a href="/wiki/Edward_VI_of_England" title="Edward VI of England">Edward VI</a>. Since Edward was then only nine years old, he could not rule directly. Instead, Henry's will designated 16 <a href="/wiki/Executor" title="Executor">executors</a> to serve on a council of regency until Edward reached the age of 18. The executors chose <a href="/wiki/Edward_Seymour,_1st_Duke_of_Somerset" title="Edward Seymour, 1st Duke of Somerset">Edward Seymour, 1st Earl of Hertford</a>, Jane Seymour's elder brother, to be <a href="/wiki/Lord_Protector" title="Lord Protector">Lord Protector</a> of the Realm. If Edward died childless, the throne was to pass to Mary, Henry VIII's daughter by Catherine of Aragon, and her heirs. If Mary's issue failed, the crown was to go to Elizabeth, Henry's daughter by Anne Boleyn, and her heirs. Finally, if Elizabeth's line became extinct, the crown was to be inherited by the descendants of Henry VIII's deceased younger sister, Mary, the Greys. The descendants of Henry's sister Margaret&#160;– the Stuarts, rulers of Scotland&#160;– were thereby excluded from the succession.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> This final provision failed when <a href="/wiki/James_VI_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="James VI of Scotland">James VI of Scotland</a> became King of England in 1603.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_image">Public image</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pastime.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Pastime.jpg/220px-Pastime.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Pastime.jpg/330px-Pastime.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Pastime.jpg/440px-Pastime.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1766" data-file-height="1346" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pastime.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Musical score of "<a href="/wiki/Pastime_with_Good_Company" title="Pastime with Good Company">Pastime with Good Company</a>", c. 1513, composed by Henry</div></div></div>
<p>Henry cultivated the image of a <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_man" class="mw-redirect" title="Renaissance man">Renaissance man</a>, and his court was a centre of scholarly and artistic innovation and glamorous excess, epitomised by the <a href="/wiki/Field_of_the_Cloth_of_Gold" title="Field of the Cloth of Gold">Field of the Cloth of Gold</a>. He scouted the country for choirboys, taking some directly from Wolsey's choir, and introduced Renaissance music into court. Musicians included <a href="/w/index.php?title=Benedict_de_Opitiis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Benedict de Opitiis (page does not exist)">Benedict de Opitiis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Sampson" title="Richard Sampson">Richard Sampson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ambrose_Lupo" title="Ambrose Lupo">Ambrose Lupo</a>, and Venetian organist <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dionisio_Memo&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Dionisio Memo (page does not exist)">Dionisio Memo</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-scarisbrick1516_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scarisbrick1516-152">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> and Henry himself kept a considerable collection of instruments. He was skilled on the lute and could play the organ, and he was a talented player of the <a href="/wiki/Virginals" title="Virginals">virginals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-scarisbrick1516_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scarisbrick1516-152">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> He could also sight read music and sing well.<sup id="cite_ref-scarisbrick1516_152-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scarisbrick1516-152">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> He was an accomplished musician, author, and poet; his best known piece of music is "<a href="/wiki/Pastime_with_Good_Company" title="Pastime with Good Company">Pastime with Good Company</a>" ("The Kynges Ballade"), and he is reputed to have written "<a href="/wiki/Greensleeves" title="Greensleeves">Greensleeves</a>" but probably did not.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Henry was an avid gambler and dice player, and he excelled at sports, especially jousting, hunting, and <a href="/wiki/Real_tennis" title="Real tennis">real tennis</a>. He was also known for his strong defence of conventional Christian piety.<sup id="cite_ref-Crofton2006a_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crofton2006a-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> He was involved in the construction and improvement of several significant buildings, including <a href="/wiki/Nonsuch_Palace" title="Nonsuch Palace">Nonsuch Palace</a>, <a href="/wiki/King%27s_College_Chapel,_Cambridge" title="King&#39;s College Chapel, Cambridge">King's College Chapel, Cambridge</a>, and Westminster Abbey in London. Many of the existing buildings which he improved were properties confiscated from Wolsey, such as <a href="/wiki/Christ_Church,_Oxford" title="Christ Church, Oxford">Christ Church, Oxford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hampton_Court_Palace" title="Hampton Court Palace">Hampton Court Palace</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Whitehall" title="Palace of Whitehall">Palace of Whitehall</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge" title="Trinity College, Cambridge">Trinity College, Cambridge</a>.
</p><p>Henry was an intellectual, the first English king with a modern humanist education. He read and wrote English, French, and Latin, and owned a large library. He annotated many books and published one of his own, and he had numerous pamphlets and lectures prepared to support the reformation of the church. Richard Sampson's <i>Oratio</i> (1534), for example, was an argument for absolute obedience to the monarchy and claimed that the English church had always been independent from Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> At the popular level, theatre and minstrel troupes funded by the crown travelled around the land to promote the new religious practices; the pope and Catholic priests and monks were mocked as foreign devils, while the glorious king was hailed as a brave and heroic defender of the true faith.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Henry worked hard to present an image of unchallengeable authority and irresistible power.<sup id="cite_ref-hibbert_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hibbert-156">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Henry was a large, well-built athlete, over 6 feet [1.8&#160;m] tall, strong, and broad in proportion, and he excelled at jousting and hunting. These were more than pastimes; they were political devices which served multiple goals, enhancing his athletic royal image, impressing foreign emissaries and rulers, and conveying his ability to suppress any rebellion. He arranged a jousting tournament at Greenwich in 1517 where he wore gilded armour and gilded horse trappings, and outfits of velvet, satin, and cloth of gold with pearls and jewels. It suitably impressed foreign ambassadors, one of whom wrote home that "the wealth and civilisation of the world are here, and those who call the English barbarians appear to me to render themselves such".<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Henry finally retired from jousting in 1536 after a heavy fall from his horse left him unconscious for two hours, but he continued to sponsor two lavish tournaments a year. He then started adding weight and lost the trim, athletic figure that had made him so handsome, and his courtiers began dressing in heavily padded clothes to emulate and flatter him. His health rapidly declined near the end of his reign.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span></h2>
<p>The power of Tudor monarchs, including Henry, was 'whole' and 'entire', ruling, as they claimed, <a href="/wiki/Divine_right_of_kings" title="Divine right of kings">by the grace of God</a> alone.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> The crown could also rely on the exclusive use of those functions that constituted the <a href="/wiki/Royal_prerogative" title="Royal prerogative">royal prerogative</a>. These included acts of diplomacy (including royal marriages), declarations of war, management of the coinage, the issue of royal pardons and the power to summon and dissolve parliament as and when required.<sup id="cite_ref-morris2_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morris2-162">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, as evident during Henry's break with Rome, the monarch worked within established limits, whether legal or financial, that forced him to work closely with both the nobility and parliament (representing the gentry).<sup id="cite_ref-morris2_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morris2-162">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cardinal_Thomas_Wolsey.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Cardinal_Thomas_Wolsey.jpg/170px-Cardinal_Thomas_Wolsey.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Cardinal_Thomas_Wolsey.jpg/255px-Cardinal_Thomas_Wolsey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Cardinal_Thomas_Wolsey.jpg/340px-Cardinal_Thomas_Wolsey.jpg 2x" data-file-width="781" data-file-height="944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cardinal_Thomas_Wolsey.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wolsey" title="Thomas Wolsey">Thomas Wolsey</a></div></div></div>
<p>In practice, Tudor monarchs used <a href="/wiki/Patronage" title="Patronage">patronage</a> to maintain a royal court that included formal institutions such as the <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Privy Council">Privy Council</a> as well as more informal advisers and confidants.<sup id="cite_ref-morris19_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morris19-163">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Both the rise and fall of court nobles could be swift: although the often-quoted figure of 72,000 executions of thieves during the last two years of his reign is inflated,<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Henry did undoubtedly execute at will, burning or beheading two of his wives, twenty peers, four leading public servants, six close attendants and friends, one cardinal (John Fisher) and numerous abbots.<sup id="cite_ref-hibbert_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hibbert-156">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Among those who were in favour at any given point in Henry's reign, one could usually be identified as a chief minister,<sup id="cite_ref-morris19_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morris19-163">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> though one of the enduring debates in the <a href="#Historiography">historiography of the period</a> has been the extent to which those chief ministers controlled Henry rather than vice versa.<sup id="cite_ref-bandf1_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bandf1-165">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> In particular, historian <a href="/wiki/G._R._Elton" class="mw-redirect" title="G. R. Elton">G. R. Elton</a> has argued that one such minister, Thomas Cromwell, led a "Tudor revolution in government" quite independent of the king, whom Elton presented as an opportunistic, essentially lazy participant in the nitty-gritty of politics. Where Henry did intervene personally in the running of the country, Elton argued, he mostly did so to its detriment.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> The prominence and influence of faction in Henry's court is similarly discussed in the context of at least five episodes of Henry's reign, including the downfall of Anne Boleyn.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From 1514 to 1529, Thomas Wolsey (1473–1530), a cardinal of the established Church, oversaw domestic and foreign policy for the young king from his position as Lord Chancellor.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Wolsey centralised the national government and extended the jurisdiction of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Conciliar_court&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Conciliar court (page does not exist)">conciliar courts</a>, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Star_Chamber" title="Star Chamber">Star Chamber</a>. The Star Chamber's overall structure remained unchanged, but Wolsey used it to provide for much-needed reform of the criminal law. The power of the court itself did not outlive Wolsey, however, since no serious administrative reform was undertaken and its role was eventually devolved to the localities.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Wolsey helped fill the gap left by Henry's declining participation in government (particularly in comparison to his father) but did so mostly by imposing himself in the King's place.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> His use of these courts to pursue personal grievances, and particularly to treat delinquents as if mere examples of a whole class worthy of punishment, angered the rich, who were annoyed as well by his enormous wealth and ostentatious living.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Following <a href="#Annulment_from_Catherine">Wolsey's downfall</a>, Henry took full control of his government, although at court numerous complex factions continued to try to ruin and destroy each other.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cromwell,Thomas(1EEssex)01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Cromwell%2CThomas%281EEssex%2901.jpg/170px-Cromwell%2CThomas%281EEssex%2901.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Cromwell%2CThomas%281EEssex%2901.jpg/255px-Cromwell%2CThomas%281EEssex%2901.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Cromwell%2CThomas%281EEssex%2901.jpg/340px-Cromwell%2CThomas%281EEssex%2901.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2768" data-file-height="3312" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cromwell,Thomas(1EEssex)01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cromwell" title="Thomas Cromwell">Thomas Cromwell</a> in 1532 or 1533</div></div></div>
<p>Thomas Cromwell (c. 1485–1540) also came to define Henry's government. Returning to England from the continent in 1514 or 1515, Cromwell soon entered Wolsey's service. He turned to law, also picking up a good knowledge of the Bible, and was admitted to <a href="/wiki/Gray%27s_Inn" title="Gray&#39;s Inn">Gray's Inn</a> in 1524. He became Wolsey's "man of all work".<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> Cromwell, driven in part by his religious beliefs, attempted to reform the body politic of the English government through discussion and consent, and through the vehicle of continuity and not outward change.<sup id="cite_ref-Elton1977_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elton1977-174">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> He was seen by many people as the man they wanted to bring about their shared aims, including Thomas Audley. By 1531, Cromwell and those associated with him were already responsible for the drafting of much legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-Elton1977_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elton1977-174">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Cromwell's first office was that of the master of the King's jewels in 1532, from which he began to invigorate the government finances.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> By this point, Cromwell's power as an efficient administrator, in a Council full of politicians, exceeded what Wolsey had achieved.<sup id="cite_ref-Elton1977a_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elton1977a-176">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Cromwell did much work through his many offices to remove the tasks of government from the Royal Household (and ideologically from the personal body of the King) and into a public state.<sup id="cite_ref-Elton1977a_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elton1977a-176">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> He did so, however, in a haphazard fashion that left several remnants, not least because he needed to retain Henry's support, his own power, and the possibility of actually achieving the plan he set out.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Cromwell made the various income streams put in place by Henry VII more formal and assigned largely autonomous bodies for their administration.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> The role of the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="King&#39;s Council">King's Council</a> was transferred to a reformed Privy Council, much smaller and more efficient than its predecessor.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> A difference emerged between the financial health of the king, and that of the country, although Cromwell's fall undermined much of his bureaucracy, which required his hand to keep order among the many new bodies and prevent profligate spending that strained relations as well as finances.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Cromwell's reforms ground to a halt in 1539, the initiative lost, and he failed to secure the passage of an <a href="/wiki/Enabling_act" title="Enabling act">enabling act</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Proclamation_by_the_Crown_Act_1539" title="Proclamation by the Crown Act 1539">Proclamation by the Crown Act 1539</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> He too was executed, on 28 July 1540.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finances">Finances</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_VIII_crown_763986.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Henry_VIII_crown_763986.jpg/220px-Henry_VIII_crown_763986.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="109" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Henry_VIII_crown_763986.jpg/330px-Henry_VIII_crown_763986.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Henry_VIII_crown_763986.jpg/440px-Henry_VIII_crown_763986.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="247" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_VIII_crown_763986.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gold <a href="/wiki/Crown_(British_coin)" title="Crown (British coin)">crown</a> of Henry VIII, minted c. 1544–1547. The reverse depicts the quartered arms of England and France.</div></div></div>
<p>Henry inherited a vast fortune and a prosperous economy from his father Henry VII, who had been frugal and careful with money. This fortune was estimated to £1,250,000 (£375&#160;million by today's standards).<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> By comparison, however, the reign of Henry was a near-disaster in financial terms. Although he further augmented his royal treasury through the seizure of church lands, Henry's heavy spending and long periods of mismanagement damaged the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Much of this wealth was spent by Henry on maintaining his court and household, including many of the building works he undertook on royal palaces. Henry hung 2,000 tapestries in his palaces; by comparison, James V of Scotland <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Royal_tapestry_collection" title="Scottish Royal tapestry collection">hung just 200</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Henry took pride in showing off his collection of weapons, which included exotic archery equipment, 2,250 pieces of land ordnance and 6,500 <a href="/wiki/Handgun#Single-shot_pistols" title="Handgun">handguns</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> Tudor monarchs had to fund all the expenses of government out of their own income. This income came from the Crown lands that Henry owned as well as from customs duties like <a href="/wiki/Tonnage_and_poundage" title="Tonnage and poundage">tonnage and poundage</a>, granted by parliament to the king for life. During Henry's reign the revenues of the Crown remained constant (around £100,000),<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> but were eroded by inflation and rising prices brought about by war. Indeed, war and Henry's dynastic ambitions in Europe exhausted the surplus he had inherited from his father by the mid-1520s.
</p><p>Whereas Henry VII had not involved Parliament in his affairs very much, Henry VIII had to turn to Parliament during his reign for money, in particular for grants of subsidies to fund his wars. The Dissolution of the Monasteries provided a means to replenish the treasury, and as a result the Crown took possession of monastic lands worth £120,000 (£36&#160;million) a year.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> The Crown had profited a small amount in 1526 when Wolsey had put England onto a gold, rather than silver, standard, and had debased the currency slightly. Cromwell debased the currency more significantly, starting in Ireland in 1540. The English pound halved in value against the Flemish pound between 1540 and 1551 as a result. The nominal profit made was significant, helping to bring income and expenditure together, but it had a catastrophic effect on the overall economy of the country. In part, it helped to bring about a period of very high inflation from 1544 onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reformation">Reformation</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Henry_VIII_of_England_and_Pope_Clement_VII.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/King_Henry_VIII_of_England_and_Pope_Clement_VII.jpg/220px-King_Henry_VIII_of_England_and_Pope_Clement_VII.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="331" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/King_Henry_VIII_of_England_and_Pope_Clement_VII.jpg/330px-King_Henry_VIII_of_England_and_Pope_Clement_VII.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/King_Henry_VIII_of_England_and_Pope_Clement_VII.jpg/440px-King_Henry_VIII_of_England_and_Pope_Clement_VII.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="3008" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Henry_VIII_of_England_and_Pope_Clement_VII.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>King Henry VIII sitting with his feet upon Pope Clement VI, 1641</div></div></div>
<p>Henry is generally credited with initiating the English Reformation – the process of transforming England from a Catholic country to a Protestant one – though his progress at the elite and mass levels is disputed,<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> and the precise narrative not widely agreed.<sup id="cite_ref-elton103_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elton103-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Certainly, in 1527, Henry, until then an observant and well-informed Catholic, appealed to the Pope for an annulment of his marriage to Catherine.<sup id="cite_ref-elton103_61-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elton103-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> No annulment was immediately forthcoming, the result in part of Charles V's control of the Papacy.<sup id="cite_ref-elton110_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elton110-191">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> The traditional narrative gives this refusal as the trigger for Henry's rejection of <a href="/wiki/Papal_supremacy" title="Papal supremacy">papal supremacy</a> (which he had previously defended), though as historian <a href="/wiki/A._F._Pollard" class="mw-redirect" title="A. F. Pollard">A. F. Pollard</a> has argued, even if Henry had not needed an annulment, Henry may have come to reject papal control over the governance of England purely for political reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In any case, between 1532 and 1537, Henry instituted a number of statutes that dealt with the relationship between king and pope and hence the structure of the nascent <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> These included the <a href="/wiki/Statute_in_Restraint_of_Appeals" title="Statute in Restraint of Appeals">Statute in Restraint of Appeals</a> (passed 1533), which extended the charge of <i><a href="/wiki/Praemunire" title="Praemunire">praemunire</a></i> against all who introduced papal bulls into England, potentially exposing them to the death penalty if found guilty.<sup id="cite_ref-bernard71_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bernard71-194">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Other acts included the <a href="/wiki/Supplication_against_the_Ordinaries" title="Supplication against the Ordinaries">Supplication against the Ordinaries</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Submission_of_the_Clergy" title="Submission of the Clergy">Submission of the Clergy</a>, which recognised Royal Supremacy over the church. The <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_Appointments_Act_1534" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecclesiastical Appointments Act 1534">Ecclesiastical Appointments Act 1534</a> required the clergy to elect bishops nominated by the Sovereign. The <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Supremacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Supremacy">Act of Supremacy</a> in 1534 declared that the King was "the only Supreme Head on Earth of the Church of England" and the <a href="/wiki/Treasons_Act_1534" title="Treasons Act 1534">Treasons Act 1534</a> made it high treason, punishable by death, to refuse the <a href="/wiki/Oath_of_Supremacy" title="Oath of Supremacy">Oath of Supremacy</a> acknowledging the King as such. Similarly, following the passage of the Act of Succession 1533, all adults in the Kingdom were required to acknowledge the Act's provisions (declaring Henry's marriage to Anne legitimate and his marriage to Catherine illegitimate) by oath;<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> those who refused were subject to imprisonment for life, and any publisher or printer of any literature alleging that the marriage to Anne was invalid subject to the death penalty.<sup id="cite_ref-bernard2005_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bernard2005-196">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> Finally, the <a href="/wiki/Act_Concerning_Peter%27s_Pence_and_Dispensations" title="Act Concerning Peter&#39;s Pence and Dispensations">Peter's Pence Act</a> was passed, and it reiterated that England had "no superior under God, but only your <a href="/wiki/His_Grace" class="mw-redirect" title="His Grace">Grace</a>" and that Henry's "imperial crown" had been diminished by "the unreasonable and uncharitable usurpations and exactions" of the Pope.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> The King had much support from the Church under Cranmer.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Henry, to Thomas Cromwell's annoyance, insisted on parliamentary time to discuss questions of faith, which he achieved through the Duke of Norfolk. This led to the passing of the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Six_Articles" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Six Articles">Act of Six Articles</a>, whereby six major questions were all answered by asserting the religious orthodoxy, thus restraining the reform movement in England.<sup id="cite_ref-elton289_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elton289-119">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> It was followed by the beginnings of a reformed <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgy</a> and of the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a>, which would take until 1549 to complete.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> The victory won by religious conservatives did not convert into much change in personnel, however, and Cranmer remained in his position.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> Overall, the rest of Henry's reign saw a subtle movement away from religious orthodoxy, helped in part by the deaths of prominent figures from before the break with Rome, especially the executions of Thomas More and John Fisher in 1535 for refusing to renounce papal authority. Henry established a new <a href="/wiki/Political_theology" title="Political theology">political theology</a> of obedience to the crown that was continued for the next decade. It reflected <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>'s new interpretation of the <a href="/wiki/Ten_commandments#Catholic_and_Lutheran_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Ten commandments">fourth commandment</a> ("Honour thy father and mother"), brought to England by <a href="/wiki/William_Tyndale" title="William Tyndale">William Tyndale</a>. The founding of royal authority on the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">Ten Commandments</a> was another important shift: reformers within the Church used the Commandments' emphasis on faith and the word of God, while conservatives emphasised the need for dedication to God and doing good. The reformers' efforts lay behind the publication of the <i><a href="/wiki/Great_Bible" title="Great Bible">Great Bible</a></i> in 1539 in English.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> Protestant Reformers still faced persecution, particularly over objections to Henry's annulment. Many fled abroad, including the influential Tyndale,<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> who was eventually executed and his body burned at Henry's behest.
</p><p>When taxes once payable to Rome were transferred to the Crown, Cromwell saw the need to assess the taxable value of the Church's extensive holdings as they stood in 1535. The result was an extensive compendium, the <i><a href="/wiki/Valor_Ecclesiasticus" title="Valor Ecclesiasticus">Valor Ecclesiasticus</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> In September of the same year, Cromwell commissioned a more general visitation of religious institutions, to be undertaken by four appointee visitors. The visitation focussed almost exclusively on the country's religious houses, with largely negative conclusions.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> In addition to reporting back to Cromwell, the visitors made the lives of the monks more difficult by enforcing strict behavioural standards. The result was to encourage self-dissolution.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> In any case, the evidence gathered by Cromwell led swiftly to the beginning of the state-enforced <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_monasteries" class="mw-redirect" title="Dissolution of the monasteries">dissolution of the monasteries</a> with all religious houses worth less than £200 vested by statute in the crown in January 1536.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> After a short pause, surviving religious houses were transferred one by one to the Crown and onto new owners, and the dissolution confirmed by a further statute in 1539. By January 1540 no such houses remained: some 800 had been dissolved. The process had been efficient, with minimal resistance, and brought the crown some £90,000 a year.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> The extent to which the dissolution of all houses was planned from the start is debated by historians; there is some evidence that major houses were originally intended only to be reformed.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> Cromwell's actions transferred a fifth of England's landed wealth to new hands. The programme was designed primarily to create a landed gentry beholden to the crown, which would use the lands much more efficiently.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> Although little opposition to the supremacy could be found in England's religious houses, they had links to the international church and were an obstacle to further religious reform.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Response to the reforms was mixed. The religious houses had been the only support of the impoverished,<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> and the reforms alienated much of the population outside London, helping to provoke the great northern rising of 1536–1537, known as the <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage_of_Grace" title="Pilgrimage of Grace">Pilgrimage of Grace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> Elsewhere the changes were accepted and welcomed, and those who clung to Catholic rites kept quiet or moved in secrecy. They would re-emerge during the reign of Henry's daughter Mary (1553–1558).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Field_Armor_of_King_Henry_VIII_of_England_(reigned_1509%E2%80%9347)_MET_DT205963.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Field_Armor_of_King_Henry_VIII_of_England_%28reigned_1509%E2%80%9347%29_MET_DT205963.jpg/170px-Field_Armor_of_King_Henry_VIII_of_England_%28reigned_1509%E2%80%9347%29_MET_DT205963.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Field_Armor_of_King_Henry_VIII_of_England_%28reigned_1509%E2%80%9347%29_MET_DT205963.jpg/255px-Field_Armor_of_King_Henry_VIII_of_England_%28reigned_1509%E2%80%9347%29_MET_DT205963.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Field_Armor_of_King_Henry_VIII_of_England_%28reigned_1509%E2%80%9347%29_MET_DT205963.jpg/340px-Field_Armor_of_King_Henry_VIII_of_England_%28reigned_1509%E2%80%9347%29_MET_DT205963.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2903" data-file-height="3722" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Field_Armor_of_King_Henry_VIII_of_England_(reigned_1509%E2%80%9347)_MET_DT205963.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Henry's Italian-made suit of armour, c. 1544. <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art" title="Metropolitan Museum of Art">Metropolitan Museum of Art</a>, New York</div></div></div>
<p>Apart from permanent garrisons at <a href="/wiki/Berwick-upon-Tweed" title="Berwick-upon-Tweed">Berwick</a>, Calais, and <a href="/wiki/Carlisle,_Cumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Carlisle, Cumbria">Carlisle</a>, England's standing army numbered only a few hundred men. This was increased only slightly by Henry.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> Henry's invasion force of 1513, some 30,000 men, was composed of <a href="/wiki/Billmen" class="mw-redirect" title="Billmen">billmen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Longbowmen" class="mw-redirect" title="Longbowmen">longbowmen</a>, at a time when the other European nations were moving to <a href="/wiki/Arquebus" title="Arquebus">hand guns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pikemen" class="mw-redirect" title="Pikemen">pikemen</a>. The difference in capability was at this stage not significant, however, and Henry's forces had new armour and weaponry. They were also supported by battlefield artillery and the <a href="/wiki/War_wagon" title="War wagon">war wagon</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> relatively new innovations, and several large and expensive siege guns.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> The invasion force of 1544 was similarly well-equipped and organised, although command on the battlefield was laid with the dukes of Suffolk and Norfolk, which in the case of the latter produced disastrous results at Montreuil.<sup id="cite_ref-Elton1977b_133-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elton1977b-133">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Henry's break with Rome incurred the threat of a large-scale French or Spanish invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-elton282_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elton282-85">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> To guard against this, in 1538, he began to build a chain of expensive, state-of-the-art defences, along Britain's southern and eastern coasts from <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a> to <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>, largely built of material gained from the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Monasteries" title="Dissolution of the Monasteries">demolition of the monasteries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> These were known as Henry VIII's <a href="/wiki/Device_Forts" title="Device Forts">Device Forts</a>. He also strengthened existing coastal defence fortresses such as Dover Castle and, at Dover, Moat Bulwark and Archcliffe Fort, which he personally visited for a few months to supervise.<sup id="cite_ref-elton282_85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elton282-85">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Wolsey had many years before conducted the censuses required for an overhaul of the system of <a href="/wiki/Militia" title="Militia">militia</a>, but no reform resulted.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> In 1538–39, Cromwell overhauled the <a href="/wiki/Muster_(military)" title="Muster (military)">shire musters</a>, but his work mainly served to demonstrate how inadequate they were in organisation.<sup id="cite_ref-elton282_85-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elton282-85">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The building works, including that at Berwick, along with the reform of the militias and musters, were eventually finished under Queen Mary.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Basire_Embarkation_of_Henry_VIII.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Basire_Embarkation_of_Henry_VIII.jpg/280px-Basire_Embarkation_of_Henry_VIII.jpg" decoding="async" width="280" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Basire_Embarkation_of_Henry_VIII.jpg/420px-Basire_Embarkation_of_Henry_VIII.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Basire_Embarkation_of_Henry_VIII.jpg/560px-Basire_Embarkation_of_Henry_VIII.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="498" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Basire_Embarkation_of_Henry_VIII.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depiction of Henry embarking at <a href="/wiki/Dover" title="Dover">Dover</a>, c.&#160;1520</div></div></div>
<p>Henry is traditionally cited as one of the founders of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-loades83_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loades83-219">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> Technologically, Henry invested in large cannon for his warships, an idea that had taken hold in other countries, to replace the smaller <a href="/w/index.php?title=Serpentine_(gun)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Serpentine (gun) (page does not exist)">serpentines</a> in use.<sup id="cite_ref-loades83_219-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loades83-219">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> He also flirted with designing ships personally&#160;– although his contribution to larger vessels, if any, is not known, it is believed that he influenced the design of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rowbarges&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Rowbarges (page does not exist)">rowbarges</a> and similar galleys.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> Henry was also responsible for the creation of a permanent navy, with the supporting anchorages and dockyards.<sup id="cite_ref-loades83_219-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loades83-219">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> Tactically, Henry's reign saw the Navy move away from boarding tactics to employ gunnery instead.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tudor_navy" title="Tudor navy">Tudor navy</a> was enlarged up to fifty ships (the <i><a href="/wiki/Mary_Rose" title="Mary Rose">Mary Rose</a></i> was one of them), and Henry was responsible for the establishment of the "council for marine causes" to specifically oversee all the maintenance and operation of the Navy, becoming the basis for the later <a href="/wiki/Admiralty" title="Admiralty">Admiralty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland">Ireland</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ireland_1450.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ireland_1450.png/220px-Ireland_1450.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ireland_1450.png/330px-Ireland_1450.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ireland_1450.png/440px-Ireland_1450.png 2x" data-file-width="466" data-file-height="582" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ireland_1450.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The division of Ireland in 1450</div></div></div>
<p>At the beginning of Henry's reign, Ireland was effectively divided into three zones: <a href="/wiki/The_Pale" title="The Pale">the Pale</a>, where English rule was unchallenged; <a href="/wiki/Leinster" title="Leinster">Leinster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Munster" title="Munster">Munster</a>, the so-called "obedient land" of Anglo-Irish peers; and the Gaelic <a href="/wiki/Connacht" title="Connacht">Connaught</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a>, with merely nominal English rule.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> Until 1513,  Henry continued the policy of his father, to allow Irish lords to rule in the king's name and accept steep divisions between the communities.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> However, upon the death of the <a href="/wiki/Gerald_FitzGerald,_8th_Earl_of_Kildare" title="Gerald FitzGerald, 8th Earl of Kildare">8th Earl of Kildare</a>, governor of Ireland, fractious Irish politics combined with a more ambitious Henry to cause trouble. When <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Butler,_7th_Earl_of_Ormond" title="Thomas Butler, 7th Earl of Ormond">Thomas Butler, 7th Earl of Ormond</a> died, Henry recognised one successor for Ormond's English, Welsh and Scottish lands, whilst in Ireland another took control. Kildare's successor, the 9th Earl, was replaced as Lord Lieutenant of Ireland by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Howard,_Earl_of_Surrey" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Howard, Earl of Surrey">Thomas Howard, Earl of Surrey</a> in 1520.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> Surrey's ambitious aims were costly, but ineffective; English rule became trapped between winning the Irish lords over with diplomacy, as favoured by Henry and Wolsey, and a sweeping military occupation as proposed by Surrey.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> Surrey was recalled in 1521, with <a href="/wiki/Piers_Butler" class="mw-redirect" title="Piers Butler">Piers Butler</a> – one of claimants to the Earldom of Ormond – appointed in his place. Butler proved unable to control opposition, including that of Kildare. Kildare was appointed chief governor in 1524, resuming his dispute with Butler, which had before been in a lull. Meanwhile, the <a href="/wiki/James_FitzGerald,_10th_Earl_of_Desmond" title="James FitzGerald, 10th Earl of Desmond">Earl of Desmond</a>, an Anglo-Irish peer, had turned his support to Richard de la Pole as pretender to the English throne; when in 1528 Kildare failed to take suitable actions against him, Kildare was once again removed from his post.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Desmond situation was resolved on his death in 1529, which was followed by a period of uncertainty. This was effectively ended with the appointment of Henry FitzRoy, Duke of Richmond and the king's son, as lord lieutenant. Richmond had never before visited Ireland, his appointment a break with past policy.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> For a time it looked as if peace might be restored with the return of Kildare to Ireland to manage the tribes, but the effect was limited and the Irish parliament soon rendered ineffective.<sup id="cite_ref-loades187_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loades187-230">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> Ireland began to receive the attention of Cromwell, who had supporters of Ormond and Desmond promoted. Kildare, on the other hand, was summoned to London; after some hesitation, he departed for London in 1534, where he would face charges of treason.<sup id="cite_ref-loades187_230-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loades187-230">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> His son, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_FitzGerald,_10th_Earl_of_Kildare" title="Thomas FitzGerald, 10th Earl of Kildare">Thomas, Lord Offaly</a> was more forthright, denouncing the king and leading a "Catholic crusade" against the king, who was by this time mired in marital problems. Offaly had the Archbishop of Dublin murdered, and besieged Dublin. Offaly led a mixture of Pale gentry and Irish tribes, although he failed to secure the support of <a href="/wiki/Lord_Darcy" title="Lord Darcy">Lord Darcy</a>, a sympathiser, or Charles V. What was effectively a civil war was ended with the intervention of 2,000 English troops – a large army by Irish standards – and the execution of Offaly (his father was already dead) and his uncles.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the Offaly revolt was followed by a determination to rule Ireland more closely, Henry was wary of drawn-out conflict with the tribes, and a royal commission recommended that the only relationship with the tribes was to be promises of peace, their land protected from English expansion. The man to lead this effort was Sir <a href="/wiki/Anthony_St_Leger_(Lord_Deputy_of_Ireland)" title="Anthony St Leger (Lord Deputy of Ireland)">Antony St Leger</a>, as <a href="/wiki/Lord_Deputy_of_Ireland" title="Lord Deputy of Ireland">Lord Deputy of Ireland</a>, who would remain into the post past Henry's death.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> Until the break with Rome, it was widely believed that Ireland was a Papal possession granted as a mere <a href="/wiki/Fiefdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Fiefdom">fiefdom</a> to the English king, so in 1541 Henry asserted England's claim to the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Kingdom of Ireland</a> free from the Papal <a href="/wiki/Lord" title="Lord">overlordship</a>. This change did, however, also allow a policy of peaceful reconciliation and expansion: the Lords of Ireland would grant their lands to the King, before being returned as fiefdoms. The incentive to comply with Henry's request was an accompanying barony, and thus a right to sit in the Irish House of Lords, which was to run in parallel with England's.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> The Irish law of the tribes did not suit such an arrangement, because the chieftain did not have the required rights; this made progress tortuous, and the plan was abandoned in 1543, not to be replaced.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span></h2>
<p>The complexities and sheer scale of Henry's legacy ensured that, in the words of Betteridge and Freeman, "throughout the centuries, Henry has been praised and reviled, but he has never been ignored".<sup id="cite_ref-bandf1_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bandf1-165">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>  Historian J.D. Mackie sums up Henry's personality and its impact on his achievements and popularity:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The respect, nay even the popularity, which he had from his people was not unmerited....He kept the development of England in line with some of the most vigorous, though not the noblest forces of the day.  His high courage – highest when things went ill – his commanding intellect, his appreciation of fact, and his instinct for rule carried his country through a perilous time of change, and his very arrogance saved his people from the wars which afflicted other lands. Dimly remembering the wars of the Roses, vaguely informed as to the slaughters and sufferings in Europe, the people of England knew that in Henry they had a great king.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>A particular focus of modern historiography has been the extent to which the events of Henry's life (including his marriages, foreign policy and religious changes) were the result of his own initiative and, if they were, whether they were the result of opportunism or of a principled undertaking by Henry.<sup id="cite_ref-bandf1_165-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bandf1-165">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> The traditional interpretation of those events was provided by historian <a href="/wiki/Albert_Pollard" title="Albert Pollard">A.F. Pollard</a>, who in 1902 presented his own, largely positive, view of the king,  lauding him, "as the king and statesman who, whatever his personal failings, led England down the road to parliamentary democracy and empire".<sup id="cite_ref-bandf1_165-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bandf1-165">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Pollard's interpretation remained the dominant interpretation of Henry's life until the publication of the doctoral thesis of G. R. Elton in 1953.
</p><p>Elton's book on <i>The Tudor Revolution in Government</i>, maintained Pollard's positive interpretation of the Henrician period as a whole, but reinterpreted Henry himself as a follower rather than a leader. For Elton, it was Cromwell and not Henry who undertook the changes in government – Henry was shrewd, but lacked the vision to follow a complex plan through.<sup id="cite_ref-bandf1_165-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bandf1-165">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Henry was little more, in other words, than an "ego-centric monstrosity" whose reign "owed its successes and virtues to better and greater men about him; most of its horrors and failures sprang more directly from [the king]".<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the central tenets of Elton's thesis have since been questioned, it has consistently provided the starting point for much later work, including that of <a href="/wiki/Jack_Scarisbrick" title="Jack Scarisbrick">J. J. Scarisbrick</a>, his student. Scarisbrick largely kept Elton's regard for Cromwell's abilities, but returned agency to Henry, who Scarisbrick considered to have ultimately directed and shaped policy.<sup id="cite_ref-bandf1_165-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bandf1-165">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> For Scarisbrick, Henry was a formidable, captivating man who "wore regality with a splendid conviction".<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> The effect of endowing Henry with this ability, however, was largely negative in Scarisbrick's eyes: to Scarisbrick the Henrician period was one of upheaval and destruction and those in charge worthy of blame more than praise.<sup id="cite_ref-bandf1_165-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bandf1-165">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Even among more recent biographers, including <a href="/wiki/David_Loades" title="David Loades">David Loades</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Starkey" title="David Starkey">David Starkey</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Guy_(historian)" title="John Guy (historian)">John Guy</a>, there has ultimately been little consensus on the extent to which Henry was responsible for the changes he oversaw or the correct assessment of those he did bring about.<sup id="cite_ref-bandf1_165-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bandf1-165">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This lack of clarity about Henry's control over events has contributed to the variation in the qualities ascribed to him: religious conservative or dangerous radical; lover of beauty or brutal destroyer of priceless artefacts; friend and patron or betrayer of those around him; chivalry incarnate or ruthless chauvinist.<sup id="cite_ref-bandf1_165-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bandf1-165">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> One traditional approach, favoured by Starkey and others, is to divide Henry's reign into two halves, the first Henry being dominated by positive qualities (politically inclusive, pious, athletic but also intellectual) who presided over a period of stability and calm, and the latter a "hulking tyrant" who presided over a period of dramatic, sometimes whimsical, change.<sup id="cite_ref-morris19_163-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morris19-163">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> Other writers have tried to merge Henry's disparate personality into a single whole; <a href="/wiki/Lacey_Baldwin_Smith" title="Lacey Baldwin Smith">Lacey Baldwin Smith</a>, for example, considered him an egotistical borderline neurotic given to great fits of temper and deep and dangerous suspicions, with a mechanical and conventional, but deeply held piety, and having at best a mediocre intellect.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Style_and_arms">Style and arms</span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r889293853/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Coat_of_Arms_of_Henry_VIII_of_England_%281509-1547%29.svg/360px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Henry_VIII_of_England_%281509-1547%29.svg.png 2x"
