srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Coat_of_Arms_of_England_%281509-1554%29.svg/270px-Coat_of_Arms_of_England_%281509-1554%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Coat_of_Arms_of_England_%281509-1554%29.svg/360px-Coat_of_Arms_of_England_%281509-1554%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1550" data-file-height="1550" /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Henry's armorial during his early reign (left) and later reign (right)</div></div></div></div>
<p>Many changes were made to the royal style during his reign. Henry originally used the style "Henry the Eighth, by the Grace of God, <a href="/wiki/List_of_monarchs_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="List of monarchs of England">King of England</a>, <a href="/wiki/English_Kings_of_France" class="mw-redirect" title="English Kings of France">France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lord_of_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord of Ireland">Lord of Ireland</a>". In 1521, pursuant to a grant from Pope Leo X rewarding Henry for his <i>Defence of the Seven Sacraments</i>, the royal style became "Henry the Eighth, by the Grace of God, King of England and France, <a href="/wiki/Fidei_defensor" class="mw-redirect" title="Fidei defensor">Defender of the Faith</a> and Lord of Ireland". Following Henry's excommunication, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_III" title="Pope Paul III">Pope Paul III</a> rescinded the grant of the title "Defender of the Faith", but an <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Parliament" title="Act of Parliament">Act of Parliament</a> (<a href="/wiki/35_Hen_8" class="mw-redirect" title="35 Hen 8">35 Hen 8</a> c 3) declared that it remained valid; and it continues in royal usage to the present day. Henry's motto was "Coeur Loyal" ("true heart"), and he had this embroidered on his clothes in the form of a heart symbol and with the word "loyal". His emblem was the <a href="/wiki/Tudor_rose" title="Tudor rose">Tudor rose</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Beaufort_portcullis" class="mw-redirect" title="Beaufort portcullis">Beaufort portcullis</a>. As king, Henry's <a href="/wiki/Heraldry" title="Heraldry">arms</a> were the same as those used by his predecessors since <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_England" title="Henry IV of England">Henry IV</a>: <i>Quarterly, Azure three <a href="/wiki/Fleur-de-lis" title="Fleur-de-lis">fleurs-de-lys</a> Or (for France) and Gules three lions passant guardant in pale Or (for England)</i>.
</p><p>In 1535, Henry added the "supremacy phrase" to the royal style, which became "Henry the Eighth, by the Grace of God, King of England and France, Defender of the Faith, Lord of Ireland and of the Church of England in Earth Supreme Head". In 1536, the phrase "of the Church of England" changed to "of the Church of England and also of <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Ireland</a>". In 1541, Henry had the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Ireland" title="Parliament of Ireland">Irish Parliament</a> change the title "Lord of Ireland" to "King of Ireland" with the <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Ireland_Act_1542" title="Crown of Ireland Act 1542">Crown of Ireland Act 1542</a>, after being advised that many Irish people regarded the Pope as the true head of their country, with the Lord acting as a mere representative. The reason the Irish regarded the Pope as their overlord was that Ireland had originally been given to King <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry II of England</a> by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Adrian_IV" title="Pope Adrian IV">Pope Adrian IV</a> in the 12th century as a feudal territory under papal overlordship. The meeting of Irish Parliament that proclaimed Henry VIII as King of Ireland was the first meeting attended by the Gaelic Irish chieftains as well as the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Irish">Anglo-Irish</a> aristocrats. The style "Henry the Eighth, by the Grace of God, King of England, France and Ireland, Defender of the Faith and of the Church of England and also of Ireland in Earth Supreme Head" remained in use until the end of Henry's reign.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although Henry would have read the verse in its Latin (vulgate) form, the translation used in the 1604 King James Bible is instructive: "And if a man shall take his brother's wife, it is an unclean thing: he hath uncovered his brother's nakedness; they shall be childless".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-88"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-88">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">On 11 July 1533 Pope Clement VII 'pronounced sentence against the King, declaring him excommunicated unless he put away the woman he had taken to wife, and took back his Queen during the whole of October next.'<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Clement died on 25 September 1534.  On 30 August 1535 the new pope, Paul III, drew up a bull of excommunication which began <i>Eius qui immobilis'<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/G._R._Elton" class="mw-redirect" title="G. R. Elton">G. R. Elton</a> puts the date the bull was made official as November 1538.<sup id="cite_ref-elton282_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elton282-85">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>  On 17 December 1538 <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_III" title="Pope Paul III">Pope Paul III</a> issued a further bull which began 'Cum redemptor noster', renewing the execution of the bull of 30 August 1535, which had been suspended in hope of his amendment.<sup id="cite_ref-Scarisbrick361_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scarisbrick361-86">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Both bulls are printed by Bishop Burnet, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/historyofreforma04burniala#page/318/mode/2up">History of the Reformation of the Church of England, 1865 edition, Volume 4, P 318ff</a> and in <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/bullarumdiploma00biligoog#page/n212/mode/2up">Bullarum, diplomatum et privilegiorum sanctorum Romanorum pontificum Taurinensis (1857) Volume VI, Page 195</a></i></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-Lockyer2014-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Lockyer2014_66-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Lockyer, Roger (22 May 2014). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=a22hAwAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA46"><i>Tudor and Stuart Britain: 1485–1714</i></a>. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">13 July</span> 2014</span>. <q>The King had no further use for Wolsey, who had failed to procure the annulment of his marriage, and he summoned Parliament in order that an act of attainder should be passed against the cardinal. The act was not needed, however, for Wolsey had also been commanded to appear before the common-law judges and answer the charge that by publishing his bulls of appointment as papal legate he had infringed the Statute of Praemunire.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Tudor+and+Stuart+Britain%3A+1485%E2%80%931714&amp;rft.pages=46&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2014-05-22&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-317-86882-8&amp;rft.aulast=Lockyer&amp;rft.aufirst=Roger&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Da22hAwAAQBAJ%26pg%3DPA46&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHenry+VIII+of+England" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-67">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFHaigh1993">Haigh 1993</a>, p.&#160;92f</span>

<li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFElton1977">Elton 1977</a>, p.&#160;116</span>

<li id="cite_note-Losch2002-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Losch2002_69-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Losch2002_69-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Losch, Richard R. (1 May 2002). <i>The Many Faces of Faith: A Guide to World Religions and Christian Traditions</i>. Wm. B. Eerdmans Publishing. p.&#160;106. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8028-0521-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8028-0521-8"><bdi>978-0-8028-0521-8</bdi></a>. <q>Henry decided to turn to the archbishop of Canterbury for the annulment, but Wolsey, recognizing that it was too late, opposed this move. Henry discharged him and appointed his friend Sir Thomas More as chancellor, confident that More would support him. More refused to make any statement for or against the annulment. When pressed to do so he resigned as the chancellor and retired to private life. He had such a reputation for integrity that his endorsement would have engendered huge support for the annulment among Parliament and the people, who loved Catherine. More's silence so angered Henry that he tried to force his hand by having him imprisoned and tried. The perfidy of the king's secretary, Thomas Cromwell, however, and the perjury of a petty bureaucrat, Richard Rich, brought about More's conviction and execution for treason in 1535. Meanwhile, a respected Cambridge scholar priest, Tomas Cranmer, supported Henry and sought support for him from the European universities.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Many+Faces+of+Faith%3A+A+Guide+to+World+Religions+and+Christian+Traditions&amp;rft.pages=106&amp;rft.pub=Wm.+B.+Eerdmans+Publishing&amp;rft.date=2002-05-01&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-8028-0521-8&amp;rft.aulast=Losch&amp;rft.aufirst=Richard+R.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHenry+VIII+of+England" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-Wilson2012-100"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Wilson2012_100-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Wilson, Derek (21 June 2012). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=j2e7Uba9Q88C&amp;pg=PT92"><i>A Brief History of the English Reformation</i></a>. Constable &amp; Robinson. p.&#160;92. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-84901-825-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-84901-825-8"><bdi>978-1-84901-825-8</bdi></a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">13 July</span> 2014</span>. <q>Cromwell, with his usual single-minded (and ruthless) efficiency, organised the interrogation of the accused, their trials and their executions. Cranmer was absolutely shattered by the 'revelation' of the queen's misdeeds. He wrote to the king expressing his difficulty in believing her guilt. But he fell into line and pronounced the annulment of Henry's second marriage on the grounds of Anne's pre-contract to another.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+Brief+History+of+the+English+Reformation&amp;rft.pages=92&amp;rft.pub=Constable+%26+Robinson&amp;rft.date=2012-06-21&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-84901-825-8&amp;rft.aulast=Wilson&amp;rft.aufirst=Derek&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Dj2e7Uba9Q88C%26pg%3DPT92&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHenry+VIII+of+England" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
